border-top:1px #aaa solid"><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_program" title="Discovery program">Discovery program</a></b></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/CONTOUR" title="CONTOUR">CONTOUR</a></i></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Messenger</b></i> (stylized as <i><b>MESSENGER</b></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Backronym" title="Backronym">backronym</a> for "<b>MErcury Surface, Space ENvironment, GEochemistry, and Ranging</b>"; the name is a reference to <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">the messenger deity of the same name</a> from <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>) was a <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic spacecraft</a> that orbited the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">planet Mercury</a> between 2011 and 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CircleMerc_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-CircleMerc-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The probe was launched aboard a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> rocket in August 2004 to study Mercury\'s chemical composition, <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mercury" title="Geology of Mercury">geology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_magnetic_field" title="Mercury\'s magnetic field">magnetic field</a>.\n</p><p>The instruments carried by <i>MESSENGER</i> were used on a complex series of <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">flybys</a> &#8211; the spacecraft flew by <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> once, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> twice, and Mercury itself three times, allowing it to decelerate relative to Mercury using minimal fuel. During its first flyby of Mercury in January 2008, <i>MESSENGER</i> became the second mission after <a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a>\'s 1975 flyby to reach Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl1-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl2_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl3_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl3-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>MESSENGER</i> entered orbit around Mercury on March 18, 2011, becoming the first spacecraft to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CircleMerc_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-CircleMerc-7">[7]</a></sup> It successfully completed its primary mission in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExMissionCompleted2013_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ExMissionCompleted2013-2">[2]</a></sup> Following two mission extensions, the <i>MESSENGER</i> spacecraft used the last of its maneuvering propellant and deorbited as planned, impacting the surface of Mercury on April 30, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150430_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150430-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div <span class="toctext">Scientific instruments</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Mission_profile"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Mission profile</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Launch_and_trajectory"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Launch and trajectory</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Earth_flyby"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Earth flyby</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Two_Venus_flybys"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Two Venus flybys</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Three_Mercury_flybys"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Three Mercury flybys</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Orbital_insertion"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Orbital insertion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Primary_science"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Primary science</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Extended_mission"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Extended mission</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Discovery_of_water,_organic_compounds_and_volcanism"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Discovery of water, organic compounds and volcanism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Solar_System_portrait"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Solar System portrait</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#End_of_mission"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">End of mission</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_overview">Mission overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>MESSENGER\'</i>s formal data collection mission began on April 4, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The primary mission was completed on March 17, 2012, having collected close to 100,000 images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuap14_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuap14-14">[14]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i> achieved 100% mapping of Mercury on March 6, 2013, and completed its first year-long extended mission on March 17, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExMissionCompleted2013_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-ExMissionCompleted2013-2">[2]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s second extended mission lasted for over two years, but as its low orbit degraded, it required reboosts to avoid impact. It conducted its final reboost burns on October 24, 2014, and January 21, 2015, before crashing into Mercury on April 30, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EclipseReboost_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-EclipseReboost-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bang_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bang-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200,000ImagesSurpassed_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-200,000ImagesSurpassed-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During its stay in Mercury orbit, <i>MESSENGER</i>\'s instruments yielded significant data, including a characterization of Mercury\'s magnetic field<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nantes5_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nantes5-18">[18]</a></sup> and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a> at the planet\'s north pole,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IceonMercury_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-IceonMercury-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> which had long been suspected on the basis of Earth-based radar data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarmonSlade1994_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarmonSlade1994-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_background">Mission background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mission background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_missions">Previous missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Previous missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1973, <a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a> was launched by NASA to make multiple flyby encounters of Venus and Mercury. Mariner 10 provided the first detailed data of Mercury, mapping 40&#8211;45% of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USATMessenger_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-USATMessenger-22">[22]</a></sup> Mariner 10\'s final flyby of Mercury occurred on March 16, 1975. No subsequent close-range observations of the planet would take place for more than 30 years.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals_for_the_mission">Proposals for the mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Proposals for the mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1998, a study detailed a proposed mission to send an orbiting spacecraft to Mercury, as the planet was at that point the least-explored of the inner planets. In the years following the Mariner 10 mission, subsequent mission proposals to revisit Mercury had appeared too costly, requiring large quantities of propellant and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_lift_launch_vehicle" title="Heavy lift launch vehicle">heavy lift launch vehicle</a>. Moreover, inserting a spacecraft into orbit around Mercury is difficult, because a probe approaching on a direct path from Earth would be accelerated by the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>\'s gravity and pass Mercury far too quickly to orbit it. However, using a trajectory designed by Chen-wan Yen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> in 1985, the study showed it was possible to seek a <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery-class</a> mission by using multiple, consecutive gravity assist, \'swingby\' maneuvers around Venus and Mercury, in combination with minor propulsive trajectory corrections, to gradually slow the spacecraft and thereby minimize propellant needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1998Design_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-1998Design-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>MESSENGER</i> mission was designed to study the characteristics and environment of Mercury from orbit. Specifically, the scientific objectives of the mission were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Objectives1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Objectives1-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>to characterize the chemical composition of Mercury\'s surface.</li>\n<li>to study the planet\'s geologic history.</li>\n<li>to elucidate the nature of the global magnetic field (<a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>).</li>\n<li>to determine the size and state of the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>.</li>\n<li>to determine the volatile inventory at the poles.</li>\n<li>to study the nature of Mercury\'s <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_design">Spacecraft design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spacecraft design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:300px;"><span class="mw-3d-wrapper" data-label="3D"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_Probe.stl"><img alt="Interactive 3D model of MESSENGER" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="5120" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/MESSENGER_Probe.stl/298px-MESSENGER_Probe.stl.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/MESSENGER_Probe.stl/447px-MESSENGER_Probe.stl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/MESSENGER_Probe.stl/596px-MESSENGER_Probe.stl.png 2x" width="298"/></a></span> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_Probe.stl" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interactive 3D model of <i>MESSENGER</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>MESSENGER</i> spacecraft was designed and built at the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a>. Science operations were managed by <a href="/wiki/Sean_Solomon" title="Sean Solomon">Sean Solomon</a> as principal investigator, and mission operations were also conducted at JHU/APL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup> The <i>MESSENGER</i> bus measured 1.85 meters (73&#160;in) tall, 1.42&#160;m (56&#160;in) wide, and 1.27&#160;m (50&#160;in) deep. The bus was primarily constructed with four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphite_fiber" title="Graphite fiber">graphite fiber</a> / <a href="/wiki/Cyanate_ester" title="Cyanate ester">cyanate ester</a> composite panels that supported the propellant tanks, the large velocity adjust (LVA) thruster, attitude monitors and correction thrusters, the antennas, the instrument pallet, and a large ceramic-cloth sunshade, measuring 2.5&#160;m (8.2&#160;ft) tall and 2&#160;m (6.6&#160;ft) wide, for passive thermal control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup> At launch, the spacecraft weighed approximately 1,100 kilograms (2,400&#160;lb) with its full load of propellant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i>\'s total mission cost, including the cost of the spacecraft\'s construction, was estimated at under US$450 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitude_control_and_propulsion">Attitude control and propulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Attitude control and propulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Main propulsion was provided by the 645&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a>, 317 sec.<a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">I<sub>sp</sub></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipropellant_rocket" title="Bipropellant rocket">bipropellant</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_tetroxide" title="Nitrogen tetroxide">nitrogen tetroxide</a>) large velocity assist (LVA) thruster. The model used was the <a href="/wiki/LEROS" title="LEROS">LEROS 1b</a>, developed and manufactured at AMPAC&#8208;ISP\'s Westcott works, in the United Kingdom. The spacecraft was designed to carry 607.8 kilograms (1,340&#160;lb) of propellant and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> pressurizer for the LVA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Four 22&#160;N (4.9&#160;lb<sub>f</sub>) <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant</a> thrusters provided spacecraft steering during main thruster burns, and twelve 4.4&#160;N (1.0&#160;lb<sub>f</sub>) monopropellant thrusters were used for <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a>. For precision attitude control, a <a href="/wiki/Reaction_wheel" title="Reaction wheel">reaction wheel</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attitude_control_system" title="Attitude control system">attitude control system</a> was also included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup> Information for attitude control was provided by <a href="/wiki/Star_tracker" title="Star tracker">star trackers</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attitude_control_(spacecraft)#Gyroscopes" title="Attitude control (spacecraft)">inertial measurement unit</a> and six <a href="/wiki/Sun_sensor" title="Sun sensor">sun sensors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The probe included two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_deep_space_transponder" title="Small deep space transponder">small deep space transponders</a> for communications with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> and three kinds of antennas: a high gain phased array whose main beam could be electronically steered in one plane, a medium-gain "fan-beam" antenna and a low gain horn with a broad pattern. The high gain antenna was used as transmit-only at 8.4&#160;GHz, the medium-gain and low gain antennas transmit at 8.4&#160;GHz and receive at 7.2&#160;GHz, and all three antennas operate with right-hand circularly polarized (RHCP) radiation. One of each of these antennas was mounted on the front of the probe facing the Sun, and one of each was mounted to the back of the probe facing away from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The space probe was powered by a two-panel <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a>/<a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_array" title="Solar array">solar array</a> providing an average of 450 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> while in Mercury orbit. Each panel was rotatable and included optical solar reflectors to balance the temperature of the array. Power was stored in a common-pressure-vessel, 23-<a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a>-hour <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93hydrogen_battery" title="Nickel&#8211;hydrogen battery">nickel&#8211;hydrogen battery</a>, with 11 vessels and two cells per vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_and_software">Computer and software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Computer and software">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The spacecraft\'s onboard computer system was contained in an Integrated Electronics Module (IEM), a device that combined core <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a> into a single box. The computer featured two <a href="/wiki/Radiation_hardening" title="Radiation hardening">radiation-hardened</a> <a href="/wiki/IBM_RAD6000" title="IBM RAD6000">IBM RAD6000s</a>, a 25&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megahertz" title="Megahertz">megahertz</a> main processor, and a 10&#160;MHz fault protection processor. For redundancy, the spacecraft carried a pair of identical IEMs. For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_storage_device" title="Data storage device">data storage</a>, the spacecraft carried two <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state</a> recorders able to store up to one <a href="/wiki/Gigabyte" title="Gigabyte">gigabyte</a> each. The IBM RAD6000 main processor collected, <a href="/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">compressed</a>, and stored data from <i>MESSENGER</i>\'s instruments for later playback to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>MESSENGER</i> used a software suite called SciBox to simulate its orbit and instruments, in order to "choreograph the complicated process of maximizing the scientific return from the mission and minimizing conflicts between instrument observations, while at the same time meeting all spacecraft constraints on pointing, data downlink rates, and onboard data storage capacity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_instruments">Scientific instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scientific instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2">Mercury Dual Imaging System (<b>MDIS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_-_MDIS.jpg"><img alt="MESSENGER - MDIS.jpg" data-file-height="241" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/MESSENGER_-_MDIS.jpg/150px-MESSENGER_-_MDIS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/MESSENGER_-_MDIS.jpg/225px-MESSENGER_-_MDIS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/MESSENGER_-_MDIS.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Included two <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> cameras, a narrow-angle camera (NAC) and a wide-angle camera (WAC) mounted to a pivoting platform. The camera system provided a complete map of the surface of Mercury at a resolution of 250 meters/pixel, and images of regions of geologic interest at 20&#8211;50 meters/pixel. Color imaging was possible only with the narrow-band filter wheel attached to the wide-angle camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MDISDescription_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-MDISDescription-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCMDIS_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCMDIS-33">[33]</a></sup><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="float:left;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Objectives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MDISDescription_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-MDISDescription-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Flyby Phase</b>\n<ul><li>Acquisition of near-global coverage at &#8776;500-meters/<a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">pixel</a>.</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multispectral" title="Multispectral">Multispectral</a> mapping at &#8776;2-kilometers/pixel.</li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Orbital Phase</b>\n<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Nadir" title="Nadir">nadir</a>-looking <a href="/wiki/Monochrome" title="Monochrome">monochrome</a> global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photomosaic" title="Photomosaic">photomosaic</a> at moderate solar <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(optics)" title="Angle of incidence (optics)">incidence angles</a> (55&#176;&#8211;75&#176;) and 250-meters/pixel or better sampling resolution.</li>\n<li>A 25&#176;-off-nadir mosaic to complement the nadir-looking mosaic for global <a href="/wiki/Stereo_imaging" title="Stereo imaging">stereo mapping</a>.</li>\n<li>Completion of the multispectral mapping begun during the flybys.</li>\n<li>High-resolution (20&#8211;50-meters/pixel) image strips across features representative of major geologic units and structures.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" <a class="external text" href="http://ode.rsl.wustl.edu/mercury/DataSetExplorer.aspx?target=mars&amp;instrumenthost=MESSENGER&amp;instrumentid=MDIS-NAC" rel="nofollow">PDS/MODE narrow-angle catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://ode.rsl.wustl.edu/mercury/DataSetExplorer.aspx?target=mars&amp;instrumenthost=MESSENGER&amp;instrumentid=MDIS-WAC" rel="nofollow">PDS/MODE wide-angle catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111007064913/http://pds-imaging.jpl.nasa.gov/Admin/resources/cd_messenger.html" rel="nofollow">PDS/PIN data catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-Ray_Spectrometer" title="Gamma-Ray Spectrometer">Gamma-Ray Spectrometer</a> (<b>GRS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_-_GRS_-_GRNS.jpg"><img alt="MESSENGER - GRS - GRNS.jpg" data-file-height="205" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/MESSENGER_-_GRS_-_GRNS.jpg/150px-MESSENGER_-_GRS_-_GRNS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/MESSENGER_-_GRS_-_GRNS.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Measured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-ray" title="Gamma-ray">gamma-ray</a> emissions from the surface of Mercury to determine the planet\'s composition by detecting certain elements (<a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur" title="Sulphur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>) to a depth of 10&#160;cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRNSDescription_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRNSDescription-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCGRNS_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCGRNS-36">[36]</a></sup>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Objectives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRNSDescription_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRNSDescription-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<ul><li>Provide surface abundances of major elements.</li>\n<li>Provide surface abundances of Fe, Si, and K, infer alkali depletion from K abundances, and provide abundance limits on H (water ice) and S (if present) at the poles.</li>\n<li>Map surface element abundances where possible, and otherwise provide surface-averaged abundances or establish upper limits.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> William Boynton / University of Arizona </small></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-geosciences.wustl.edu/missions/messenger/grns.htm" rel="nofollow">PDS/GSN data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://ode.rsl.wustl.edu/mercury/DataSetExplorer.aspx?target=mars&amp;instrumenthost=MESSENGER&amp;instrumentid=GRS" rel="nofollow">PDS/MODE GRS data catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2">Neutron Spectrometer (<b>NS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg"><img alt="MESSENGER - NS.jpg" data-file-height="186" data-file-width="357" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg/150px-MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg/225px-MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg/300px-MESSENGER_-_NS.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Determined the hydrogen mineral composition to a depth of 40&#160;cm by detecting low-energy neutrons resulting from the collision of cosmic rays with the minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRNSDescription_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-GRNSDescription-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCGRNS_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCGRNS-36">[36]</a></sup>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Objectives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRNSDescription_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-GRNSDescription-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<ul><li>Establish and map the abundance of hydrogen over most of the northern hemisphere of Mercury.</li>\n<li>Investigate the possible presence of water ice within and near permanently shaded craters near the north pole.</li>\n<li>Provide secondary evidence to aid in interpreting GRS measured gamma-ray line strengths in terms of elemental abundances.</li>\n<li>Outline surface domains at the base of both northern and southern cusps of the magnetosphere where the solar wind can implant hydrogen in surface material.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> William Boynton / University of Arizona </small></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-geosciences.wustl.edu/missions/messenger/grns.htm" rel="nofollow">PDS/GSN data catalog</a>,  <a class="external text" href="http://ode.rsl.wustl.edu/mercury/DataSetExplorer.aspx?target=mars&amp;instrumenthost=MESSENGER&amp;instrumentid=NS" rel="nofollow">PDS/MODE NS data catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2"><a href="/wiki/X-ray_spectroscopy" title="X-ray spectroscopy">X-Ray Spectrometer</a> (<b>XRS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_-_XRS.jpg"><img alt="MESSENGER - XRS.jpg" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="115" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/MESSENGER_-_XRS.jpg/225px-MESSENGER_-_XRS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/MESSENGER_-_XRS.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Mapped mineral composition within the top millimeter of the surface on Mercury by detecting <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> from <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur" title="Sulphur">sulphur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, and iron, in the 1&#8211;10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">keV</a> range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XRSDescription_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-XRSDescription-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCXRS_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCXRS-38">[38]</a></sup>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Objectives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XRSDescription_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-XRSDescription-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<ul><li>Determine the history of the formation of Mercury</li>\n<li>Characterize the composition of surface elements by measuring the X-ray emissions induced by the incident <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flux" title="Solar flux">solar flux</a>.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> George Ho / APL </small></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2"><a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a> (<b>MAG</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_-_MAG.jpg"><img alt="MESSENGER - MAG.jpg" data-file-height="188" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="113" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Measured the magnetic field around Mercury in detail to determine the strength and average position of the field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAGDescription_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAGDescription-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCMAG_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCMAG-40">[40]</a></sup>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Objectives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAGDescription_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-MAGDescription-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<ul><li>Investigate the structure of Mercury\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Mercury" title="Magnetosphere of Mercury">magnetic field</a> and its interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.</li>\n<li>Characterize the geometry and time variability of the magnetospheric field.</li>\n<li>Detect wave-particle interactions with the magnetosphere.</li>\n<li>Observe magnetotail dynamics, including phenomena possibly analogous to substorms in the Earth\'s magnetosphere.</li>\n<li>Characterize the magnetopause structure and dynamics.</li>\n<li>Characterize field-aligned currents that link the planet with the magnetosphere.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> Mario Acuna / NASA Goddard Space Flight Center </small></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=MESSMAG&amp;title=Magnetometer_%28MAG%29" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2">Mercury Laser Altimeter (<b>MLA</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSEBGER_-_MLA.jpg"><img alt="MESSEBGER - MLA.jpg" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Provided detailed information regarding the height of landforms on the surface of Mercury by detecting the light of an infrared laser as the light bounced off the surface. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLADescription_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLADescription-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCMLA_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLADescription-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<ul><li>Provide a high-precision topographic map of the high northern latitude regions.</li>\n<li>Measure the long-wavelength topographic features at mid-to-low northern latitudes.</li>\n<li>Determine topographic profiles across major geologic features in the northern hemisphere.</li>\n<li>Detect and quantify the planet\'s forced physical librations by tracking the motion of large-scale topographic features as a function of time.</li>\n<li>Measure the surface reflectivity of Mercury at the MLA operating wavelength of 1,064 nanometers.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> David Smith / GSFC </small></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2">Mercury Atmospheric and Surface Composition Spectrometer (<b>MASCS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div width="107"/></a></div>\n</td>\n<td>Determined the characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">tenuous atmosphere</a> surrounding Mercury by measuring ultraviolet light emissions, and ascertained the prevalence of iron and titanium minerals on the surface by measuring the <a href="/wiki/Reflectance" title="Reflectance">reflectance</a> of infrared light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MASCSDescription_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-MASCSDescription-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCMASCS_44-0"><a processes that generate and maintain the exosphere.</li>\n<li>Determine the relationship between exospheric and surface composition.</li>\n<li>Search for polar deposits of volatile material, and determine how are the accumulation of these deposits are related to exospheric processes.</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> William McClintock / catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2">Energetic Particle and Plasma Spectrometer (<b>EPPS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" and their relationships to external drivers and their internal conditions.</li>\n<li>Measure interplanetary plasma properties in cruise and in Mercury catalog</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #f2f2f2">Radio Science (<b>RS</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Measured the gravity of Mercury and the state Mercury\'s gravity to quantify the amplitude of Mercury\'s <a href="/wiki/Libration" title="Libration">libration</a>.</li>\n<li>Provide precise measurements of the range of the <i>MESSENGER</i> of mission</i></center>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_trajectory">Launch and trajectory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Launch and trajectory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>MESSENGER</i> probe was launched on August 3, 2004 at 06:15:56 UTC by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">NASA</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_17" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 17">Space Launch Complex 17B</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> in Florida, aboard a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II 7925</a> launch vehicle. The complete burn sequence lasted 57 minutes bringing the spacecraft into a heliocentric orbit, with a final velocity of 10.68&#160;km/s (6.64&#160;miles/s) and sending the probe into a 7.9 billion-kilometer trajectory that took 6 years, 7 months and 16 days before its orbital insertion on March 18, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MESSENGERLaunch_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-MESSENGERLaunch-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Traveling to Mercury requires an extremely large velocity change (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a></i>) because Mercury\'s orbit is deep in the Sun\'s <a href="/wiki/Gravity_well" title="Gravity well">gravity well</a>.  On a direct course from Earth to Mercury, a spacecraft is constantly accelerated as it falls toward the Sun, and will arrive at Mercury with a velocity too high to achieve orbit without excessive use of fuel. For planets with an atmosphere, such as <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, spacecraft can minimize their fuel consumption upon arrival by using friction with the atmosphere to enter orbit (<a href="/wiki/Aerocapture" title="Aerocapture">aerocapture</a>), or can briefly fire their rocket engines to enter into orbit followed by a reduction of the orbit by <a href="/wiki/Aerobraking" title="Aerobraking">aerobraking</a>. However, the tenuous <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">atmosphere of Mercury</a> is far too thin for these maneuvers.  Instead, <i>MESSENGER</i> extensively used <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> maneuvers at Earth, Venus, and Mercury to reduce the speed relative to Mercury, then used its large rocket engine to enter into an elliptical orbit around the planet.  The multi-flyby process greatly reduced the amount of propellant necessary to slow the spacecraft, but at the cost of prolonging the trip by many years and to a total distance of 4.9 billion miles.\n</p><p>Several planned thruster firings en route to Mercury were unnecessary, because these fine course adjustments were performed using solar radiation pressure acting on MESSENGER\'s solar panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> To further minimize the amount of necessary propellant, the spacecraft orbital insertion targeted a highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical_orbit" title="Elliptical orbit">elliptical orbit</a> around Mercury.\n</p><p>The elongated orbit had two other benefits:  It allowed the spacecraft time to cool after the times it was between the hot surface of Mercury and the Sun, and also it allowed the spacecraft to measure the effects of <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> and the magnetic fields of the planet at various distances while still allowing close-up measurements and photographs of the surface and exosphere.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a trajectory of the <i>MESSENGER</i> orbiter.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_flyby">Earth flyby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Earth flyby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">Earth observation satellite</a></div>\n<p><i>MESSENGER</i> performed an Earth <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">flyby</a> one year after launch, on August 2, 2005, with the closest approach at 19:13 <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a> at an altitude of 2,347 kilometers (1,458 statute miles) over central <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>. On December 12, 2005, a 524-second-long burn (Deep-Space Maneuver or DSM-1) of the large thruster adjusted the trajectory for the upcoming Venus flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Earth flyby, the <i>MESSENGER</i> team imaged the Earth and Moon using MDIS and checked the status of several other instruments observing the atmospheric and surface compositions and testing the magnetosphere and determining that all instruments tested were working as expected. This calibration period was intended to ensure accurate interpretation of data when the spacecraft entered orbit around Mercury. Ensuring that the instruments functioned correctly at such an early stage in the mission allowed opportunity for multiple minor errors to be dealt with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MGREarthFlyby_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-MGREarthFlyby-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Earth flyby was used to investigate the <a href="/wiki/Flyby_anomaly" title="Flyby anomaly">flyby anomaly</a>, where some spacecraft have been observed to have trajectories that differ slightly from those predicted.  However no anomaly was observed in MESSENGER\'s flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a during its Earth flyby.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:22.5px auto;"><a anot.ogv">Full-size video</a>).\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Two_Venus_flybys">Two Venus flybys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Two Venus flybys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Venus" title="Exploration of Venus">Exploration of Venus</a></div>\n<p>On October 24, 2006 at 08:34 UTC, <i>MESSENGER</i> encountered Venus at an altitude of 2,992 kilometers (1,859&#160;mi). During the encounter, <i>MESSENGER</i> passed behind Venus and entered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superior_conjunction" title="Superior conjunction">superior conjunction</a>, a period when Earth was on the exact opposite side of the Solar System, with the Sun inhibiting radio contact. For this reason, no scientific observations were conducted during the flyby. Communication with the spacecraft was reestablished in late November and performed a deep space maneuver on December 12, to correct the trajectory to encounter Venus in a second flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MGRVenusFlyby1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-MGRVenusFlyby1-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 5, 2007, at 23:08 UTC, <i>MESSENGER</i> performed a second flyby of Venus at an altitude of 338&#160;km (210&#160;mi), for the greatest velocity reduction of the mission. During the encounter, all instruments were used to observe Venus and prepare for the following Mercury encounters. The encounter provided visible and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">near-infrared</a> imaging data of the upper <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">atmosphere of Venus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> and X-ray <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometry</a> of the upper atmosphere were also recorded, to characterize the composition. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">Venus Express</a> was also orbiting during the encounter, providing the first opportunity for simultaneous measurement of particle-and-field characteristics of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div <i>MESSENGER</i> on its first flyby of the planet in 2006.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: class="gallerytext">\n<p>Sequence of images as <i>MESSENGER</i> departs after the second flyby of the planet.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_Mercury_flybys">Three Mercury flybys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Three Mercury flybys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mercury" title="Exploration of Mercury">Exploration of Mercury</a></div>\n<p><i>MESSENGER</i> made a flyby of Mercury on January 14, 2008 (making its closest approach of 200&#160;km above the surface of Mercury at 19:04:39 <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a>), followed by a second flyby on October 6, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl1_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl1-9">[9]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i> executed a final flyby on September 29, 2009, further slowing down the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl3_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl3-11">[11]</a></sup> Sometime during the closest approach of the last flyby, the spacecraft entered <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode_(spacecraft)" title="Safe mode (spacecraft)">safe mode</a>. Although this had no effect on the trajectory necessary for later orbit insertion, it resulted in the loss of science data and images that were planned for the outbound leg of the fly-by. The spacecraft had fully recovered by about seven hours later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> One last deep space maneuver, DSM-5, was executed on November 24, 2009, at 22:45 UTC to provide the required velocity change for the scheduled Mercury orbit insertion on March 18, 2011, marking the beginning of the orbital mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: first high-resolution color Wide Angle Camera image of Mercury acquired by <i>MESSENGER</i>.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div href="/wiki/Rachmaninoff_(crater)" title="Rachmaninoff (crater)">Rachmaninoff crater</a>, from third flyby\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_insertion">Orbital insertion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Orbital insertion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The thruster maneuver to insert the probe into Mercury\'s orbit began at 00:45&#160;UTC on March 18, 2011. The maneuver lasted about 15 minutes, with confirmation that the craft was in Mercury orbit received at 01:10&#160;UTC on March 18 (9:10 PM, March 17 EDT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MGROrbitInsert_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-MGROrbitInsert-54">[54]</a></sup> Mission lead engineer Eric Finnegan indicated that the spacecraft had achieved a near-perfect orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BSun_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-BSun-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>MESSENGER\'</i>s orbit was highly elliptical, taking it within 200 kilometers (120&#160;mi) of Mercury\'s surface and then 15,000&#160;km (9,300&#160;mi) away from it every twelve hours. This orbit was chosen to shield the probe from the heat radiated by Mercury\'s hot surface. Only a small portion of each orbit was at a low altitude, where the spacecraft was subjected to radiative heating from the hot side of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif/300px-Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif/450px-Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d1/Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_MESSENGER_trajectory_around_Mercury.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>MESSENGER</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory around <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> from 15 March 2011 to 30 December 2014<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i>MESSENGER</i></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a></span></div></div></div></div>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGERannouncement.jpg"><img alt="Charles Bolden and colleagues wait for news from MESSENGER." data-file-height="422" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/MESSENGERannouncement.jpg/160px-MESSENGERannouncement.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/MESSENGERannouncement.jpg/241px-MESSENGERannouncement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/MESSENGERannouncement.jpg/320px-MESSENGERannouncement.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_F._Bolden,_Jr." title="Charles F. Bolden, Jr.">Charles Bolden</a> and colleagues wait for news from the <i>MESSENGER</i> probe.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>A simplified chart showing the path of <i>MESSENGER</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s orbital insertion.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_science">Primary science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Primary science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After <i>MESSENGER\'</i>s orbital insertion, an eighteen-day commissioning phase took place. The supervising personnel switched on and tested the craft\'s science instruments to ensure they had completed the journey without damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrbitInsertionPressKit_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-OrbitInsertionPressKit-65">[65]</a></sup> The commissioning phase "demonstrated that the spacecraft and payload [were] all operating nominally, notwithstanding Mercury\'s challenging environment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The primary mission began as planned on April 4, 2011, with <i>MESSENGER</i> orbiting Mercury once every twelve hours for an intended duration of twelve Earth months, the equivalent of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_day" title="Solar day">solar days</a> on Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-31">[31]</a></sup> Principal Investigator Sean Solomon, then of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institution_of_Washington" title="Carnegie Institution of Washington">Carnegie Institution of Washington</a>, said: "With the beginning today of the primary science phase of the mission, we will be making nearly continuous observations that will allow us to gain the first global perspective on the innermost planet. Moreover, as solar activity steadily increases, we will have a front-row seat on the most dynamic magnetosphere&#8211;atmosphere system in the Solar System."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 5, 2011, the scientific results obtained by <i>MESSENGER</i> during its first six terrestrial months in Mercury\'s orbit were presented in a series of papers at the European Planetary Science Congress in <a href="/wiki/Nantes" title="Nantes">Nantes</a>, France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nantes5_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nantes5-18">[18]</a></sup> Among the discoveries presented were the unexpectedly high concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> found on Mercury\'s nightside, and the fact that Mercury\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> is offset far to the north of the planet\'s center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nantes5_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nantes5-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:19.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG"><img alt="A Monochrome view of Mercury from MESSENGER" data-file-height="1017" data-file-width="1413" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG/175px-MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG/263px-MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG/350px-MESSENGERmercurylimb.PNG 2x" width="175"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Monochrome" title="Monochrome">monochrome</a> image of Mercury from <i>MESSENGER</i>.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stevenson_crater_(MESSENGER).png"><img alt="Crater Stevenson, with crater chains class="gallerytext">\n<p>Crater Stevenson, with two perpendicular <a href="/wiki/Secondary_crater" title="Secondary crater">secondary</a> <a href="/wiki/Crater_chain" title="Crater chain">crater chains</a> running through its center.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div href="/wiki/Polar_projection" title="Polar projection">polar projection</a> of Mercury.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGERridges.png"><img alt="A close snapshot of Ridges near the South Pole" data-file-height="2967" data-file-width="3564" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/MESSENGERridges.png/162px-MESSENGERridges.png" Mercury\'s south pole.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tectonically_Active_Planet_Mercury.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1748" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="135" class="gallerytext">\n<p>A <i>MESSENGER</i> image of Mercury shows previously undetected fault scarps&#8212; cliff-like landforms resembling stairs that are small enough that scientists believe they are geologically young. This shows that Mercury is still contracting, and that Earth is not the only tectonically active Solar System planet.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extended_mission">Extended mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Extended mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In November 2011, NASA announced that the <i>MESSENGER</i> mission would be extended by one year, allowing the spacecraft to observe the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">solar maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extended2013_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Extended2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Its extended mission began on March 17, 2012, and continued until March 17, 2013. Between April 16 and April 20, 2012, <i>MESSENGER</i> carried out a series of thruster manoeuvres, placing it in an eight-hour orbit to conduct further scans of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2012, NASA reported that <i>MESSENGER</i> had discovered both <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a> and organic compounds in permanently shadowed craters in Mercury\'s north pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IceonMercury_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-IceonMercury-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In February 2013, NASA published the most detailed and accurate 3D map of Mercury to date, assembled from thousands of images taken by <i>MESSENGER</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i> completed its first extended mission on March 17, 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExMissionCompleted2013_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-ExMissionCompleted2013-2">[2]</a></sup> and its second lasted until April 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200,000ImagesSurpassed_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-200,000ImagesSurpassed-17">[17]</a></sup> In November 2013, <i>MESSENGER</i> was among the numerous space assets that imaged <a href="/wiki/Comet_Encke" title="Comet Encke">Comet Encke</a> (2P/Encke) and <a href="/wiki/Comet_ISON" title="Comet ISON">Comet ISON</a> (C/2012 S1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131206_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131206-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARXIV-20140508_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARXIV-20140508-72">[72]</a></sup> As its orbit began to decay in early 2015, <i>MESSENGER</i> was able to take highly detailed close-up photographs of ice-filled craters and other landforms at Mercury\'s north pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DyingGifts_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-DyingGifts-73">[73]</a></sup>  After the mission was completed, review of the radio ranging data provided the first measurement of the rate of mass loss from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:16px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19247-Mercury-NPolarRegion-Messenger20150316.jpg"><img alt="" radiating from it.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a (blue).\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span id="Discovery_of_water.2C_organic_compounds_and_volcanism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_water,_organic_compounds_and_volcanism">Discovery of water, organic compounds and volcanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Discovery of water, organic compounds and volcanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On July 3, 2008, the <i>MESSENGER</i> team announced that the probe had discovered large amounts of water present in Mercury\'s <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>, which was an unexpected finding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_society_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary_society-77">[77]</a></sup> In the later years of its mission, <i>MESSENGER</i> also provided visual evidence of past volcanic activity on the surface of Mercury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volcanism_2011_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Volcanism_2011-78">[78]</a></sup> as well as evidence for a liquid iron <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">planetary core</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_society_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-planetary_society-77">[77]</a></sup> The probe also constructed the most detailed and accurate maps of Mercury to date, and furthermore discovered carbon-containing <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> and water ice inside permanently shadowed craters near the north pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_2015_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-results_2015-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="font-size:95%; line-height:130%">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg/200px-Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg/300px-Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg/400px-Gravity_Anomalies_on_Mercury.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Mass_concentration_(astronomy)" title="Mass concentration (astronomy)">Mass concentrations</a> (red; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caloris_Basin" title="Caloris Basin">Caloris Basin</a> at center, <a href="/wiki/Sobkou_Planitia" title="Sobkou Planitia">Sobkou Planitia</a> at right), detected via gravity anomalies, provide evidence for subsurface structure and evolution.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3752" data-file-width="3750" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg/200px-PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg/300px-PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg/400px-PIA19420-Mercury-NorthHem-Topography-MLA-Messenger-20150416.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Northern hemisphere topography from <a href="/wiki/MESSENGER#Scientific_instruments" title="MESSENGER">MLA</a> data shows a 10&#160;km vertical range: high (red); low (purple).\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg/200px-Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg/300px-Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg/400px-Unmasking_the_Secrets_of_Mercury.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER#Scientific_instruments" title="MESSENGER">MASCS</a> spectral scan of Mercury\'s surface.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:60.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2179" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg/200px-PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg/300px-PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg/400px-PIA19411-Mercury-WaterIce-Radar-MDIS-Messenger-20150416.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mercury#High-albedo_polar_patches_and_possible_presence_of_ice" title="Geology of Mercury">Water ice</a> (yellow) in permanently shaded craters of Mercury\'s north polar region\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_System_portrait">Solar System portrait</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Solar System portrait">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_Portrait_(MESSENGER)" title="Family Portrait (MESSENGER)">Family Portrait (MESSENGER)</a></div>\n<p>On February 18, 2011, a portrait of the Solar System was published on the <i>MESSENGER</i> website.  The mosaic contained 34 images, acquired by the MDIS instrument during November 2010.  All the planets were visible with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, due to their vast distances from the Sun. The <i>MESSENGER</i> "family portrait" was intended to be complementary to the <a href="/wiki/Family_Portrait_(Voyager)" title="Family Portrait (Voyager)">Voyager family portrait</a>, which was acquired from the outer Solar System by <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> on February 14, 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APLPortrait_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-APLPortrait-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg/800px-MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg/1200px-MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg/1600px-MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MESSENGER_Solar_System_Family_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><i>MESSENGER</i> captured a near-complete portrait of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> during November 2010.</center></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mission">End of mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: End of mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>\nAfter running out of <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> for course adjustments, <i>MESSENGER</i> entered its expected terminal phase of orbital decay in late 2014. The spacecraft\'s operation was extended by several weeks by exploiting its remaining supply of helium gas, which was used to pressurize its propellant tanks, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pressurant2015_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pressurant2015-81">[81]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i> continued studying Mercury during its decay period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_2015_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_2015-3">[3]</a></sup> The spacecraft crashed onto the surface of Mercury on April 30, 2015, at 3:26&#160;p.m. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">EDT</a> (19:26 GMT), at a velocity of 14,080&#160;km/h (8,750&#160;mph), probably creating a crater in the planet\'s surface approximately 16&#160;m (52&#160;ft) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bang_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bang-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com.end_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-space.com.end-82">[82]</a></sup> The spacecraft was estimated to have impacted at 54.4&#176; N, 149.9&#176; W on <a href="/wiki/Suisei_Planitia" title="Suisei Planitia">Suisei Planitia</a>, near the crater <a href="/wiki/Jan%C3%A1%C4%8Dek_(crater)" title="Jan&#225;&#269;ek (crater)">Jan&#225;&#269;ek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The crash occurred on the side of the planet not visible from Earth, and thus was not detected by any observers or instruments. NASA confirmed the end of the <i>MESSENGER</i> mission at 3:40&#160;p.m. EDT (19:40 GMT) after NASA\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> failed to detect the spacecraft\'s reemergence from behind Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com.end_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-space.com.end-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="1030" decoding="async" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg/600px-PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg/900px-PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19449-PlanetMercury-MESSENGER-Images-First-20110329-Last-20150430.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><i>MESSENGER\'</i>s first (March 29, 2011) and last (April 30, 2015) images from Mercury\'s orbit (<a href="/wiki/File:Details_of_MESSENGER%27s_Impact_Location.jpg" title="File:Details of MESSENGER\'s Impact Location.jpg">impact details</a>).</center></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MESSENGER&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/BepiColombo" title="BepiColombo">BepiColombo</a></i>, a European-Japanese mission to Mercury which launched on 19 October 2018 and will enter orbit in December 2025</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mercury" title="Exploration of Mercury">Exploration of Mercury</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner program</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Stamatios_Krimigis" title="Stamatios Krimigis">Stamatios Krimigis</a>, a NASA physicist and key contributor to the mission<div style="clear:both;"></div></li></ul>\n<h2><span
